        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.4c17755"><meta property="og:title" content="Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/acsami.4c17755.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="O3-type layered oxides are considered promising cathode materials for next-generation high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, they face challenges, such as low rate capacity and poor cycling stability, which arise from structural deformation, sluggish Na+ diffusion kinetics, and interfacial side reactions. Herein, a synergistic substitution strategy for transitional and interstitial sites was adopted to improve the structure stability and Na+ diffusion kinetics of the O3-type NaNi0.2Fe0.4Mn0.4O2. Simulation results indicate that Co3+/B3+ codoping effectively lowers the Na+ migration energy barrier. In addition, the synergistic effect of Co3+/B3+ codoping provides ultralow lattice strain during repeated Na+ deintercalation/intercalation. In situ characterization verified that the complex phase transformation during charge and discharge was suppressed, thereby significantly improving the structural stability. At 1 and 3 C, the capacity retention of the modified O3–Na(Ni0.2Fe0.4Mn0.4)0.96Co0.04B0.02O2 (NFMCB) improved from 29.6% and 1.7% to 86.7% and 88.6% after 200 cycles, respectively. Even at 10 C, it could still produce 107.2 mAh·g–1. Furthermore, full cells assembled with this material and commercial hard carbon exhibit a high energy density of 316.2 Wh·kg–1 and a capacity retention of 80.8% after 200 cycles at 1 C. It is expected that this strategy will facilitate the commercialization of O3-type layered oxides."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/acsami.4c17755.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93188602b82f54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188602b82f54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '79cf1632-cb17-4ad1-a7fa-1b97fa175676'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188602b82f54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="93188602b82f54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Synergistic Effect of Anchoring Transitional/Interstitial Sites on Boosting Structural and Electrochemical Stability of O3-Type Layered Sodium Oxides", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c17755", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val">-</div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/medium/am4c17755_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c17755&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">O3-type layered oxides are considered promising cathode materials for next-generation high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, they face challenges, such as low rate capacity and poor cycling stability, which arise from structural deformation, sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, and interfacial side reactions. Herein, a synergistic substitution strategy for transitional and interstitial sites was adopted to improve the structure stability and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of the O3-type NaNi<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Simulation results indicate that Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> codoping effectively lowers the Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barrier. In addition, the synergistic effect of Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> codoping provides ultralow lattice strain during repeated Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation/intercalation. In situ characterization verified that the complex phase transformation during charge and discharge was suppressed, thereby significantly improving the structural stability. At 1 and 3 C, the capacity retention of the modified O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFMCB) improved from 29.6% and 1.7% to 86.7% and 88.6% after 200 cycles, respectively. Even at 10 C, it could still produce 107.2 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, full cells assembled with this material and commercial hard carbon exhibit a high energy density of 316.2 Wh·kg<sup>–1</sup> and a capacity retention of 80.8% after 200 cycles at 1 C. It is expected that this strategy will facilitate the commercialization of O3-type layered oxides.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your

                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Sodium-ion%20batteries">Sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Synergistic+Substitution&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Synergistic%20substitution">Synergistic substitution</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Structure+Stability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Structure%20stability">Structure stability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Diffusion+Kinetics&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Diffusion%20kinetics">Diffusion kinetics</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cycling+Stability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Cycling%20stability">Cycling stability</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In large-scale energy storage systems, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) offer promising application prospects due to their cost advantages and sustainability. The cathode material is critical in determining the operating voltage, energy density, and cycle life of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Layered transition metal oxides, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM stands for transition metal), are a subject of significant research interest due to their simple preparation process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> Although O3-type Na(Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub><i>y</i></sub>Mn<sub><i>z</i></sub>)O<sub>2</sub> (0.1 &lt; <i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i> &lt; 1) exhibits a high theoretical capacity, its low practical capacity and poor cycling stability limit its application in energy storage devices. The harmful multiphase transitions and sluggish kinetics during the extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> lead to rapid capacity decay and deterioration of the rate capability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8−10)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To solve these problems, traditional strategies have employed surface coating and element doping. Surface coating is considered to be an effective method for protecting cathode materials from electrolyte erosion. However, it cannot regulate the unfavorable phase transition of the bulk phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 11 to 14">(11−14)</a> Element doping stabilizes the bulk structure and surface state of the cathode material and improves the electrochemical performance of the cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> Doping active or inactive cations (such as Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Cr<sup>3+</sup>, and Cu<sup>2+</sup>) at TM sites can inhibit structural transformation by optimizing the component ratio. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 18 to 23">(18−23)</a> Alternatively, the bond strength of the transition metal–oxygen (TM-O) can be adjusted through dopants to reduce the slip of TM layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Owing to strong electronegativity of some anions (e.g., F<sup>–</sup> and Cl<sup>–</sup>), the shielding effect accelerates ion transport and provides additional sodium storage sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a> These strategies have been confirmed by many studies to significantly improve the performance of the O3-type cathode materials. However, it is not satisfactory to achieve comprehensive enhancement of electrochemical performances befitting from a single element doped due to the inherent structural characteristics of the electrode material. Multisite doping is designed to integrate the advantages of different doping elements, which integrate the synergistic effect in mitigating structural degradation. For example, Chen et al. reported a P2-layered lithium dual-site-substituted Na<sub>0.7</sub>Li<sub>0.03</sub>Mg<sub>0.15</sub>Li<sub>0.07</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode with poor cyclic stability, which was significantly improved because the volumetric strain approached zero during cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Wu et al. reported K/Ti dual-site-doping of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, which significantly reduces the formation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies and provides ultralow lattice strain during repeated Na<sup>+</sup> de/insertion processes, thus enhancing structural stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The usually introduced cations with higher relative atomic mass and relatively lower electrochemical activity reduce the mass ratio of the active transition metal elements, which inevitably sacrifices the specific capacity of the electrode. In addition, the modifying mechanism of cation/anion doping for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation from the O3-type layered oxide materials is crucial to meet the actual requirements of SIB. It has been reported that the replaced parts of Ni<sup>2+</sup> with Co<sup>3+</sup> show higher electrochemical activity, which improves the reversibility of phase transition under a high cutoff voltage of 4.0–4.2 V and is beneficial to crystal structure stability, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> while the local modification of grain boundaries in crystal structure has also demonstrated a contribution to structural stability. For instance, B<sup>3+</sup> owing to smaller ionic radius is more likely to occupy the tetrahedral gap to construct the B–O bond with higher bond energy than Ni–O, Fe–O, and Mn–O bonds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 32 to 34">(32−34)</a> The B–O bond strengthens the TM-O bond, shrinks the TM layers, and relieves the strain. In previous work, we have found that 2 mol % B doping effectively improves the structural stability of O3-type Na<sub>0.85</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material, but the substitution with electrochemically inert B inevitably sacrifices capacity of the electrode. Therefore, it is more ideal to develop a modification strategy that improves the structural stability, does not sacrifice the cathode capacity, and also has faster storage and reaction kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we proposed a cosubstitution of transitional sites and interstitial sites by Co<sup>3+</sup> and B<sup>3+</sup> in the prepared codoped O3–Na (Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material. The structural stability would be improved without sacrificing capacity by introducing the electrochemically active component Co<sup>3+</sup>, and Co<sup>3+</sup> doping ensures a highly ordered crystal structure and improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. In addition, B<sup>3+</sup> is used to improve framework stability and mitigate overoxidation. As a result, the obtained sample exhibited a high reversible capacity, excellent cycling stability, and rate capability. The modified cathode material was assembled in full cells, which exhibited a specific energy after a long-term cycle, indicating the significant advantage of the codoping synergistic effect and potential application value.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p">O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.01, 0.02, 0.04), O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>Co<sub><i>y</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>y</i> = 0.02, 0.04, 0.06), and O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were synthesized by sol–gel and high-temperature solid-phase methods.</div><div class="NLM_p">For the preparation of O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 2 g of Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> (commercial) was added to a certain volume of 0.1 M NaBH<sub>4</sub> (Aladdin, 99%) anhydrous ethanol solution and stirred for 3 h. The mixed solution was filtered, washed, and vacuum-dried to obtain the modified precursor. The modified precursor and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, 99%, 5 mol % excess) were mixed by ball milling. The mixed material was precalcined at 450 °C for 5 h, calcined at 900 °C for 15 h at a heating rate of 2 °C/min, and naturally cooled to 200 °C to obtain the modified sample. Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are labeled as NFMB-1, NFMB-2, and NFMB-3, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">For the preparation of O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>Co<sub><i>y</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 2 g of Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> (commercial) was added to a certain volume of 0.006 M Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin, 99%) in anhydrous ethanol solution, and the mixture was stirred for 3 h. The subsequent washing, drying, sodium mixing, and calcination processes are identical with those used for preparing the O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> material. The synthesized materials Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.98</sub>Co<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.94</sub>Co<sub>0.06</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were labeled as NFMC-1, NFMC-2, and NFMC-3, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p last">For the preparation of O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 2 g of Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> (commercial) was added to a certain volume of 0.006 M Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin, 99%) anhydrous ethanol solution and stirred continuously for 0.5 h. Then, 0.1 M NaBH<sub>4</sub> (Aladdin, 99%) anhydrous ethanol solution was added, and the mixture was stirred continuously for 3 h. The subsequent washing, drying, sodium mixing, and calcination processes are identical with those used for preparing the O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> material. The synthesized material O3–Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is labeled NFMCB, and the pristine sample is labeled NFM.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction (XRD, Rigaku) was employed to characterize the phase of the samples and analyze their unit cell parameters. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Philips, FEI Quanta 200 FEG) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-ARM200F) were utilized to obtain microscopic morphological information. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Scientific, Escalab 250 XI) was employed to measure the valence states of the elements in the samples. High-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) images were obtained using a spherical aberration-corrected transmission electron microscope (AC-TEM, JEM-ARM-200F), and the elemental distribution map at the atomic scale was determined by using the integrated X-ray photoelectron spectrometer. Inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES, Shimadzu, ICPS-8100) was employed to analyze the elemental composition of the samples.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of the material were assessed by using a CR2032 coin cell. For the half-cell configuration, sodium (Na) foil was employed as the counter electrode, while a hard carbon electrode was utilized for the full cell configuration. The mass ratio of active material, conductive agent (SP), and binder (PVDF) in both the positive and negative electrode slurries was 8:1:1. The positive electrode slurry was coated onto aluminum foil and then cut into discs with a diameter of 14 mm, yielding a loading of 3.6 mg·cm<sup>–2</sup> after drying. A 1 mol·L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution in propylene carbonate (PC) was used as the electrolyte with a glass fiber membrane (GF/D) serving as the separator. The battery was assembled in an inert glovebox to prevent exposure to moisture or oxygen.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The half-cell (2.0–4.2 V) and full-cell (2.0–4.0 V) were subjected to constant current charge and discharge tests using a battery test system (LAND CT2001A). The half-cell was also subjected to cyclic voltammetry (CV, 0.1 mV·s<sup>–1</sup>) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS, 10<sup>–2</sup>–10<sup>6</sup> Hz, 5 mV) using an electrochemical workstation (DH7000C). The half-cell was evaluated using galvanostatic intermittent titration techniques (GITT) at a rate of 0.2 C with pulse and relaxation durations of 10 and 60 min, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Theoretical Simulation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In constructing the model, bulk structures of Na<sub>27</sub>Fe<sub>11</sub>Mn<sub>11</sub>Ni<sub>5</sub>O<sub>54</sub> (NFM) and Na<sub>27</sub>Fe<sub>10</sub>CoMn<sub>11</sub>Ni<sub>5</sub>O<sub>54</sub>B (NFMCB) were developed. Structural optimizations were conducted using Density Functional Theory (DFT) with the Vienna <i>Ab initio</i> Simulation Package (VASP) employing the projector augmented wave (PAW) method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) functional, combined with DFT-D3 correction, was utilized for the exchange functional. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> A plane-wave cutoff energy of 450 eV was established during the optimization. Lattice size optimization for NFM and NFMCB involved Brillouin Zone integration with a Monkhorst–Pack 2 × 2 × 1 k-point mesh. Self-consistent calculations were performed with a convergence energy threshold of 10<sup>–5</sup> eV, optimizing equilibrium geometries and lattice constants with a maximum stress of 0.02 eV·Å<sup>–1</sup> per atom. The spin polarization method was applied to characterize the magnetism of NFM and NFMCB with Hubbard U corrections introduced for the 3d orbitals of Fe, Mn, Ni, and Co (<i>U</i><sub>Fe</sub> = 4.0 eV, <i>U</i><sub>Mn</sub> = 4.0 eV, <i>U</i><sub>Ni</sub> = 6.4 eV, and <i>U</i><sub>Co</sub> = 3.3 eV). The Climbing Image Nudged Elastic Band (CINEB) method was employed to determine the transition state and migration path of Na in NFM and NFMCB, using energy and stress criteria set at 10<sup>–7</sup> eV and 0.05 eV·Å<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">NaNi<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM), Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFMB-2), Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFMC-2), and Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFMCB) cathodes were determined by ICP-AES, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. To study the effect of Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> doping on the crystal structure, all samples were tested by XRD, and the spectra are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. All samples had a typical O3-type layered structure with a space group of <i>R3̅m</i>, and the sharp diffraction peaks proved that all samples had good crystallinity. Compared with the NFM sample, the (003) diffraction peaks of all the modified samples shifted to smaller angles, especially the NFMCB sample, which exhibited the largest shift. According to the Bragg equation, the interlayer spacing of all modified samples was expanded. Furthermore, NFM and NFMCB exhibit lower <i>R</i><sub><i>wp</i></sub> and <i>R</i><sub><i>p</i></sub> values, suggesting that their fitting results possess higher credibility (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>A,B). It can be seen that Ni, Fe, Mn, and Co occupy the octahedral sites (3b) in the TM layer owing to their similar ionic radii, while B occupies the tetrahedral interstitial sites of the O stacking. After Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> doping, the lattice parameter <i>a</i> decreased from 2.961 to 2.956 Å and lattice parameter <i>c</i> increased from 16.217 to 16.281 Å (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S2 and S3</a>). On the one hand, because the ionic radius of Co<sup>3+</sup> (65 Å) is slightly smaller than those of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (69 Å) and Mn<sup>4+</sup> (67 Å), Co occupies the 3b site and causes the TM layer to shrink. On the other hand, because the B–O bond energy is much higher than that of TM-O, the entry of B<sup>3+</sup> into the tetrahedral gap will shrink the TM layer, which in turn leads to the expansion of the Na layer. Therefore, Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> doping causes the overall crystal structure to expand. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>C, the interlayer spacings of the Na and TM layers of NFMCB are 3.285 and 2.208 Å, respectively, and the interlayer spacings of NFM are 2.932 Å (Na layer) and 2.447 Å (TM layer), respectively. The contraction of the TM layer may enhance the structural stability by inhibiting deformation and slippage of the TM layer during the extraction and insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. However, the expansion of the Na layer tends to relieve the lattice strain and enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics owing to the reduced screening effect of the positively charged Na<sup>+</sup> on the adjacent O layer. Therefore, Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> doping enhances the electrochemical performance by improving the structural stability and Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, all samples showed an irregular morphology and were stacked by nanoplates. In the HR-TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>D,E) and AC-TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>F,G), the (101) interplanar spacing of NFMCB (0.22 nm) is slightly smaller than that of NFM (0.23 nm), whereas the (003) interplanar spacing of NFMCB (0.52 nm) is slightly larger than that of NFM (0.50 nm), which is consistent with the Rietveld refinement results. Elemental mapping analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>H) showed that the NFMCB particles were enriched with Na/Ni/Fe/Mn/Co/O/B elements and were evenly distributed. The atomic-level energy spectrum of the NFMCB is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>I. The distribution of Co atoms was consistent with that of Ni/Fe/Mn atoms, showing a defect distribution trend, indicating that Co atoms were successfully doped into the TM site. Because B is a lightweight element and the doping amount is small, it is not shown in the atomic-level energy spectrum. XPS was used to analyze the Co/B elements in NFMCB. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, when the etching depth reaches 72 nm, the characteristic peaks of Co–O and B–O remain detectable. In summary, the experimental results show that Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> was successfully doped into the NFM lattice.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/medium/am4c17755_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD Rietveld refinement results for (A) NFM and (B) NFMB. (C) Schematic diagrams of the crystal structure model of NFM and NFMCB. HR-TEM images of (D, F) NFM and (E, G) NFMCB. (H) HAADF-STEM image and corresponding EDX elemental mappings of Na, Ni, Fe, Mn, Co, O, and B for NFMCB. (I) EDS maps of Na, Ni, Fe, Mn, Co, O, and B at single-atom resolution for NFMCB.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c17755&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate the promotion of the Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> synergistic doping effect on the electrochemical performance of NFM, GCD tests were performed on all samples. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>A,B and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>, the initial discharge capacity of the NFMCB sample at 0.1 C was 147.3 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup>, which was higher than that of NFMC-2 (146.1 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup>) and NFMB-2 (145.7 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup>). The initial discharge capacity of the NFM sample was the lowest, at only 142.4 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup>. The higher capacity of NFMC-2 was attributed to the electrochemical activity of Co<sup>3+</sup>, which increased the initial discharge capacity. Interestingly, B<sup>3+</sup> is not electrochemically active but can increase the initial discharge capacity. The charge–discharge curve of the NFMB-2 electrode in the voltage range of 2.0–2.5 V is flatter, indicating that the introduction of B<sup>3+</sup> activates the redox activity of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> to a certain extent. Furthermore, three pairs of redox peaks (3.27/2.74, 3.66/3.36, and 4.16/3.76 V) were observed in the CV curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>D) for NFM, which correspond to the three voltage platforms in the charge/discharge profiles. These three pairs of redox peaks are related to the Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup>, and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox reactions. Furthermore, the positions of the three pairs of redox peaks for NFM shifted significantly during cycling, indicating poor structural stability. When B<sup>3+</sup> was introduced into NFM, NFMB-2 had a wider peak shape, the curves overlapped best in the first three cycles, and a weak redox peak appeared in the voltage range of 2.0–2.5 V, which was consistent with the initial charge/discharge curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5A</a>). With the introduction of Co<sup>3+</sup>, it can be observed that the CV curves have a higher overlap and the reduction peak intensity at 3 V increases, indicating that Co<sup>3+</sup> can promote the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> at a lower voltage (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5B</a>). Benefiting from Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> doping, the overlapping CV curves of NFMCB showed highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion behavior, ensuring good structural stability.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/medium/am4c17755_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of NFM (A) and NFMCB (B) at 0.1 C. CV curves of NFM (D) and NFMCB (E) at a flow rate of 0.1 mV·s<sup>–1</sup>. (C, F) Comparison of the rate performances between NFM and NFMCB. GITT curves and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of NFM (G) and NFMCB (H). (I, J) Comparison of cyclic stability performances for NFM, NFMB-2, NFMC-2, and NFMCB at 1 and 3 C, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c17755&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">All samples were tested at rates ranging from 0.1 to 10 C. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>C,F and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6A,B</a>, all modified samples exhibited excellent rate performance, especially the NFMCB sample, with reversible capacities of 142.05, 133.26, 128.37, 123.43, 120.24, 115.36, and 107.20 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5, and 10 C, respectively. It is noteworthy that, after high-current charge and discharge, NFMCB retains a capacity of 142.98 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C, which remains almost unchanged compared to its initial capacity, indicating its ability to rapidly undergo Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. In contrast, the capacity of the NFM electrode decayed rapidly and was only 69.82 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 C. The diffusion behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> in the NFM and NFMCB electrodes was analyzed by the GITT. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>G,H shows the GITT curves and corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient curves for the second cycle of the NFM and NFMCB electrodes, respectively. The average sodium ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) of NFMCB calculated by GITT is 1.97 × 10<sup>–8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>·s<sup>–1</sup>, which is higher than the average <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of the NFM positive electrode material (7.45 × 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>·s<sup>–1</sup>), indicating that the NFMCB electrode has better kinetic characteristics. In addition, the cycling performance was evaluated at 1 and 3 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>I,G). The reversible capacity of NFMCB after 200 cycles at 1 C was maintained at 111.8 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup>, with a capacity retention of 91.3%, indicating its superior stability, which is significantly better than those of NFMB-2 (82.1%), NFMC-2 (81.9%), and NFM (29.6%). In addition, the NFMCB maintained a capacity of 110.3 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 3 C, with a capacity retention of 88.6%. In contrast, NFM showed a sharp drop in capacity after only 20 cycles, and the voltage decayed rapidly, which may be related to the harmful structural transformation. The average output voltage and energy density of NFM and NFMCB at 1 C with the cycle numbers are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. Notably, the average output voltage of the NFMB is more stable than that of the NFM, and the energy density is also higher. The d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of NFM and NFMCB are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. The curve of NFMCB almost overlaps and does not shift significantly, whereas the redox peak of NFM attenuates and shifts significantly.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics were further evaluated using CV. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, the peak current exhibits a good linear relationship with the square root of the scan rate. It should be noted that, the larger the slope, the faster is the Na<sup>+</sup> migration. NFMCB had a higher slope than NFM during both charge and discharge. In addition, EIS results further verified that NFMCB had good Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> shows the Nyquist plots of the NFM and NFMCB. The fitted <i>R</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> and <i>R</i><sub><i>ct</i></sub> values are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>. It can be seen that the <i>R</i><sub><i>ct</i></sub> value of NFMCB after 100 cycles only increased by 11.16 Ω, which is much smaller than that of NFM (2171.70 Ω), indicating that NFMCB has a lower migration barrier, which is beneficial for enhancing the reaction kinetics during charge and discharge. In summary, through the double substitution strategy, we successfully designed an O3-type layered cathode material with excellent rate performance. The difference in radius between Co<sup>3+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> is small, and occupying the TM site inhibits the ordering and phase transition of the TM layer. B<sup>3+</sup> occupies the interstitial site, and sharing oxygen with TM can strengthen the TM-O bond, stabilize the crystal structure, and inhibit the phase transition caused by the slip of the TM layer.</div><div class="NLM_p">To observe the effect of Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> doping on the structural evolution of the cathode during the entire charge–discharge process, in situ XRD experiments were conducted on NFM, NFMB-2, NFMC-2, and NFMCB electrodes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12</a> show contour plots of the in situ XRD pattern of the electrode. In addition, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a> also shows the changing trend of the corresponding <i>c</i>-axis parameters. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A, the NFM electrode underwent O3 → P3 → O3′. As Na<sup>+</sup> is removed, the (003) diffraction peak splits and gradually decreases in intensity until it disappears completely, indicating that NFM undergoes an O3 → P3 phase transition. With further Na<sup>+</sup> extraction, the O3 phase of the oligomer is fully transformed into the P3 phase. When charged to approximately 4.0 V, the (003) diffraction peak shifts to a higher angle, accompanied by a decrease in intensity, reflecting the formation of the O3′ phase. This stage leads to a significant shrinkage of the interlayer spacing, resulting in degradation of the NFM electrode structure and reduced electrochemical reversibility. After embedding Na<sup>+</sup>, NFM transforms from the O3′ phase to the P3 phase and finally transforms into the O3 phase through the O3/P3 dual-phase coexistence, and the corresponding Δ<i>c</i> change rate is as high as 6.2% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13A</a>). Under 4.0 V, NFMB-2 undergoes the transformation O3 → P3, and there is an obvious dual-phase process. Above 4.0 V, a mixed phase of O3′ and P3 appears, and the shrinkage of the interlayer spacing is suppressed. Therefore, the Δ<i>c</i> shrinkage decreased to 4.6% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13B</a>). When Co<sup>3+</sup> was introduced into the TM sites, a reversible O3 → P3 phase transition was observed in the NFMC-2 cathode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>C). An obvious dual-phase process was observed, and the Δ<i>c</i> shrinkage was reduced to 3.4% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13C</a>). Interestingly, the O3 → P3 phase transition of the NFMCB electrode does not appear in an obvious two-phase coexistence but is similar to a solid solution reaction process. The structural evolution during the cycle was highly reversible with a Δ<i>c</i> value of only 1.1% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13D</a>). A lower volumetric strain stabilizes the lattice structure of the cathode and creates a less inactive insulating material. The results show that Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> doping can effectively alleviate the unfavorable multiphase transition.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/medium/am4c17755_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. In situ XRD patterns of (A) NFM, (B) NFMB-2, (C) NFMC-2, and (D) NFMCB cathodes during the charge–discharge process between 2.0 and 4.2 V at 0.1 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c17755&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the enhanced electrochemical performance and structural stability of NFMCB, DFT calculations were conducted to examine the impact of Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> codoping on electronic structure and Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barriers. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>B reveals that the total density of states (TDOS) for the NFM sample primarily arises from the Mn-3d and the O-2p orbitals near the Fermi level. In contrast, the NFMCB sample’s electronic states near the Fermi level predominantly involve Mn-3d, Co-2p, and O-2p orbitals, with B atoms contributing minimally (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>C). Both samples exhibit semiconductor characteristics, with small energy gaps. However, Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> codoping reduces the energy gap from 0.95 to 0.77 eV, approaching semimetallic behavior, indicating improved conductivity in NFMCB. Additionally, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path in NFMCB, shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>A, indicates a preference for two-dimensional diffusion between TM layers. The calculations demonstrate that under complete Na concentration limitation the Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barrier is lower in NFMCB (0.55 eV) compared to NFM (0.73 eV). DFT results confirm that the dual anchoring effect of Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> significantly enhances Na<sup>+</sup> migration, elucidating the structural stability of NFMCB during cycling.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/medium/am4c17755_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (A) The schematic structure of NFMCB with the framework is shown, highlighting the selected migration paths used to calculate the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> migration. DOS of (B) NFM and (C) NFMCB. (D) The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers of NFM and NFMCB.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c17755&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To verify the practicality of NFMCB, full cells were assembled using this electrode and a commercial hard carbon electrode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>A shows the schematic structure of the NFMCB//HC full cell. In the advanced full-cell assembly, the HC negative electrode is pre-sodiumized by electrochemical charge and discharge, which is of great significance for subsequent matching with the NFMCB electrode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>B shows the GCD curve of the NFMCB//HC full cell at 0.1 C. The NFMCB//HC full cell can obtain a high energy density of 316.2 Wh·kg<sup>1–</sup> (based on the total mass of the positive electrode active material). In addition, the reversible capacity of the full cell at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5, and 10 C is 103.3, 100.6, 98.3, 95.8, 94.3, 89.9, and 79.7 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>C). The full cell exhibited an excellent cycling performance with a capacity retention of 80.8% after 200 cycles at 1 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>D). Compared to the currently reported layered oxide cathode materials, the full cell has an advantage in terms of capacity retention (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>E and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/suppl_file/am4c17755_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref22 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref22 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 18, 22 and 38 to 41">(18,22,38−41)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/medium/am4c17755_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (A) Schematic illustration of the NFMCB//HC full cell. (B) GCD profiles of the NFMCB//HC full cell. (C) Rate capability and (D) cyclic performance of the full cell. (E) Comparison of the full cell with other reported sodium-ion full-cell configurations in terms of cycling performance.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c17755/asset/images/large/am4c17755_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c17755&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we proposed a Co<sup>3+</sup>/B<sup>3+</sup> codoping strategy to stabilize O3-type NaNi<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes. The synergistic effect of Co<sup>3+</sup> and B<sup>3+</sup> in the NFM system lowers the migration energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup>, promotes the diffusion, inhibits the harmful P3 → O3′ phase transition, significantly improves the dynamic properties of the NFM system, and provides structural stability. Therefore, the prepared Na(Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>)<sub>0.96</sub>Co<sub>0.04</sub>B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibits impressive electrochemical properties, including high reversible capacity (147.3 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C), excellent capacity retention (91.3% and 88.6% after 200 cycles at 1 and 3 C for half-cell, 80.8% after 200 cycles at 1 C for full cell), and rate performance (97 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 C). Rational multielement doping strategies can enhance the structural stability of O3-type layered oxides, thereby accelerating their commercialization.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A High-Rate, Durable Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Sb-Doped O3-Type Ni/Mn-Based Layered Oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yuan, Tao; Li, Siqing; Sun, Yuanyuan; Wang, Jeng-Han; Chen, An-Jie; Zheng, Qinfeng; Zhang, Yixiao; Chen, Liwei; Nam, Gyutae; Che, Haiying; Yang, Junhe; Zheng, Shiyou; Ma, Zi-Feng; Liu, Meilin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18058-18070</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">O3-Type layered oxides are widely studied as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high theor. capacities.  However, their rate capability and durability are limited by tortuous Na+ diffusion channels and complicated phase evolution during Na+ extn./insertion.  Here we report our findings in unravelling the mechanism for dramatically enhancing the stability and rate capability of O3-NaNi0.5Mn0.5-xSbxO2 (NaNMS) by substitutional Sb doping, which can alter the coordination environment and chem. bonds of the transition metal (TM) ions in the structure, resulting in a more stable structure with wider Na+ transport channels.  Furthermore, NaNMS nanoparticles are obtained by surface energy regulation during grain growth.  The synergistic effect of Sb doping and nanostructuring greatly reduces the ionic migration energy barrier while increasing the reversibility of the structural evolution during repeated Na+ extn./insertion.  An optimized NaNMS-1 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 212.3 mAh g-1 at 0.2 C and 74.5 mAh g-1 at 50 C with minimal capacity loss after 100 cycles at a low temp. of -20°C.  Such electrochem. performance is superior to most of the reported layered oxide cathodes used in rechargeable SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202303773"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Surface Engineering Through In Situ Construction of Co<sub>x</sub>B-Spinel Dual Coating Layers for High-Voltage Stable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.
